</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Cognitive" title="Template:Cognitive"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Cognitive" title="Template talk:Cognitive"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Cognitive&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Memory</b> is the faculty of the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> by which <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> or <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> is <a href="/wiki/Encoding_(memory)" title="Encoding (memory)">encoded</a>, stored, and retrieved when needed. It is the retention of information over time for the purpose of influencing future action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> If <a href="/wiki/Foresight_(psychology)" title="Foresight (psychology)">past events</a> could not be remembered, it would be impossible for language, relationships, or <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">personal identity</a> to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Memory loss is usually described as <a href="/wiki/Forgetting" title="Forgetting">forgetfulness</a> or <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staniloiu_Markowitsch_2012_p._3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staniloiu_Markowitsch_2012_p.-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_2014_pp._959–972_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2014_pp._959–972-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ortega-de_San_Luis_Ryan_p=dmm035055_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega-de_San_Luis_Ryan_p=dmm035055-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staniloiu_Markowitsch_pp._101–129_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staniloiu_Markowitsch_pp._101–129-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spiegel_Smith_Wade_Cherukuru_2017_pp._2691–2703_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spiegel_Smith_Wade_Cherukuru_2017_pp._2691–2703-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer_Larkina_pp._907–924_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer_Larkina_pp._907–924-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Memory is often understood as an <a href="/wiki/Information_processing" title="Information processing">informational processing</a> system with explicit and implicit functioning that is made up of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_processor" title="Sensory processor">sensory processor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Short-term_memory" title="Short-term memory">short-term</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">working</a>) memory, and <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_2007-9">[9]</a></sup> This can be related to the <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a>.
The sensory processor allows information from the outside world to be sensed in the form of chemical and physical stimuli and attended to various levels of focus and intent. Working memory serves as an encoding and retrieval processor. Information in the form of stimuli is encoded in accordance with explicit or implicit functions by the working memory processor. The working memory also retrieves information from previously stored material.  Finally, the function of long-term memory is to store data through various categorical models or systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_2007_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_2007-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">Declarative, or explicit, memory</a> is the conscious storage and recollection of data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf_Schacter_1985_pp._501–18_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf_Schacter_1985_pp._501–18-10">[10]</a></sup> Under declarative memory resides <a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">semantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a>. Semantic memory refers to memory that is encoded with specific meaning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup> while episodic memory refers to information that is encoded along a spatial and temporal plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schacter_Addis_pp._773–786_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schacter_Addis_pp._773–786-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schacter_Addis_Buckner_2007_pp._657–661_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schacter_Addis_Buckner_2007_pp._657–661-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szpunar_2010_pp._142–162_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szpunar_2010_pp._142–162-13">[13]</a></sup> Declarative memory is usually the primary process thought of when referencing memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Implicit_memory" title="Implicit memory">Non-declarative, or implicit, memory</a> is the unconscious storage and recollection of information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783-14">[14]</a></sup> An example of a non-declarative process would be the unconscious learning or retrieval of information by way of <a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">procedural memory</a>, or a priming phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tulving_Schacter_pp._301–6_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tulving_Schacter_pp._301–6-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Priming_(psychology)" title="Priming (psychology)">Priming</a> is the process of <a href="/wiki/Subliminal_stimuli" title="Subliminal stimuli">subliminally</a> arousing specific responses from memory and shows that not all memory is consciously activated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tulving_Schacter_pp._301–6_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tulving_Schacter_pp._301–6-15">[15]</a></sup> whereas procedural memory is the slow and gradual learning of skills that often occurs without conscious attention to learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Foerde_Knowlton_Poldrack_pp._11778–11783-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Memory is not a perfect processor, and is affected by many factors. The ways by which information is encoded, stored, and retrieved can all be corrupted. Pain, for example, has been identified as a physical condition that impairs memory, and has been noted in animal models as well as chronic pain patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The amount of attention given new stimuli can diminish the amount of information that becomes encoded for storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Also, the storage process can become corrupted by physical damage to areas of the brain that are associated with memory storage, such as the hippocampus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squire_pp._12711–12716_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Squire_pp._12711–12716-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squire_Wixted_2011_pp._259–288_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Squire_Wixted_2011_pp._259–288-21">[21]</a></sup> Finally, the retrieval of information from long-term memory can be disrupted because of decay within long-term memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eysenck_2012_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eysenck_2012-2">[2]</a></sup> Normal functioning, decay over time, and brain damage all affect the accuracy and capacity of the memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_Zhong_Lu_Wang_p=e0146356_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li_Zhong_Lu_Wang_p=e0146356-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett_Rivera_Rypma_2013_pp._20–32_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett_Rivera_Rypma_2013_pp._20–32-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sensory_memory">Sensory memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sensory memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sensory_memory" title="Sensory memory">Sensory memory</a></div>
<p>Sensory memory holds information, derived from the senses, less than one second after an item is perceived. The ability to look at an item and remember what it looked like with just a split second of observation, or memorization, is the example of sensory memory. It is out of cognitive control and is an automatic response. With very short presentations, participants often report that they seem to "see" more than they can actually report. The first precise experiments exploring this form of sensory memory were conducted by <a href="/wiki/George_Sperling" title="George Sperling">George Sperling</a> (1963)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>  using the "partial report paradigm." Subjects were presented with a grid of 12 letters, arranged into three rows of four. After a brief presentation, subjects were then played either a high, medium or low tone, cuing them which of the rows to report. Based on these partial report experiments, Sperling was able to show that the capacity of sensory memory was approximately 12 items, but that it degraded very quickly (within a few hundred milliseconds). Because this form of memory degrades so quickly, participants would see the display but be unable to report all of the items (12 in the "whole report" procedure) before they decayed. This type of memory cannot be prolonged via rehearsal.
</p><p>Three types of sensory memories exist. <a href="/wiki/Iconic_memory" title="Iconic memory">Iconic memory</a> is a fast decaying store of visual information, a type of sensory memory that briefly stores an image that has been perceived for a small duration. <a href="/wiki/Echoic_memory" title="Echoic memory">Echoic memory</a> is a fast decaying store of auditory information, also a sensory memory that briefly stores sounds that have been perceived for short durations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson_2010_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson_2010-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haptic_memory" title="Haptic memory">Haptic memory</a> is a type of sensory memory that represents a database for touch stimuli.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Short-term_memory">Short-term memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Short-term memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Short-term_memory" title="Short-term memory">Short-term memory</a></div>
<p>Short-term memory is also known as <b>working memory</b>. Short-term memory allows recall for a period of several seconds to a minute without rehearsal. Its capacity, however, is very limited. In 1956, <a href="/wiki/George_Armitage_Miller" title="George Armitage Miller">George A. Miller</a> (1920–2012), when working at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Laboratories</a>, conducted experiments showing that the store of short-term memory was 7±2 items. (Hence, the title of his famous paper, <a href="/wiki/The_Magical_Number_Seven,_Plus_or_Minus_Two" title="The Magical Number Seven, Plus or Minus Two">"The Magical Number 7±2."</a>) Modern perspectives  estimate the capacity of short-term memory to be lower, typically on the order of 4–5 items,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowan_2019_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowan_2019-27">[27]</a></sup> or argue for a more flexible limit based on information instead of items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ma_2014_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_2014-28">[28]</a></sup> Memory capacity can be increased through a process called <a href="/wiki/Chunking_(psychology)" title="Chunking (psychology)">chunking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_1956_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_1956-29">[29]</a></sup> For example, in recalling a ten-digit <a href="/wiki/Telephone_number" title="Telephone number">telephone number</a>, a person could chunk the digits into three groups: first, the area code (such as 123), then a three-digit chunk (456), and, last, a four-digit chunk (7890). This method of remembering telephone numbers is far more effective than attempting to remember a string of 10 digits; this is because we are able to chunk the information into meaningful groups of numbers. This is reflected in some countries'  tendencies to display telephone numbers as several chunks of two to four numbers.
</p><p>Short-term memory is believed to rely mostly on an acoustic code for storing information, and to a lesser extent on a visual code. Conrad (1964)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conrad_(1964)_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conrad_(1964)-30">[30]</a></sup> found that test subjects had more difficulty recalling collections of letters that were acoustically similar, e.g., E, P, D. Confusion with recalling acoustically similar letters rather than visually similar letters implies that the letters were encoded acoustically. Conrad's (1964) study, however, deals with the encoding of written text; thus, while memory of written language may rely on acoustic components, generalizations to all forms of memory cannot be made.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_memory">Long-term memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Long-term memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">Long-term memory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5016" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="375" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg/170px-Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg/255px-Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg/340px-Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memory-Warner-Highsmith.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olin_Levi_Warner" title="Olin Levi Warner">Olin Levi Warner</a>, <i>Memory</i> (1896). Library of Congress <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Building" title="Thomas Jefferson Building">Thomas Jefferson Building</a>, Washington, D.C.</div></div></div>
<p>The storage in sensory memory and short-term memory generally has a strictly limited capacity and duration, which means that information is not retained indefinitely. By contrast, while the total capacity of long-term memory has yet to be established, it can store much larger quantities of information. Furthermore, it can store this information for a much longer duration, potentially for a whole life span. For example, given a random seven-digit number, one may remember it for only a few seconds before forgetting, suggesting it was stored in short-term memory. On the other hand, one can remember telephone numbers for many years through repetition; this information is said to be stored in long-term memory.
</p><p>While short-term memory encodes information acoustically, long-term memory encodes it semantically: Baddeley (1966)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_1966_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_1966-31">[31]</a></sup> discovered that, after 20 minutes, test subjects had the most difficulty recalling a collection of words that had similar meanings (e.g. big, large, great, huge) long-term. Another part of long-term memory is episodic memory, "which attempts to capture information such as 'what', 'when' and 'where<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton_1998_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton_1998-32">[32]</a></sup> With episodic memory, individuals are able to recall specific events such as birthday parties and weddings.
</p><p>Short-term memory is supported by transient patterns of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Neuronal_communication&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Neuronal communication (page does not exist)">neuronal communication</a>, dependent on regions of the <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal lobe</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Dorsolateral_prefrontal_cortex" title="Dorsolateral prefrontal cortex">dorsolateral prefrontal cortex</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Parietal_lobe" title="Parietal lobe">parietal lobe</a>. Long-term memory, on the other hand, is maintained by more stable and permanent changes in neural connections widely spread throughout the brain. The <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> is essential (for learning new information) to the consolidation of information from short-term to long-term memory, although it does not seem to store information itself. It was thought that without the hippocampus new memories were unable to be stored into long-term memory and that there would be a very short <a href="/wiki/Attention_span" title="Attention span">attention span</a>, as first gleaned from patient <a href="/wiki/Henry_Molaison" title="Henry Molaison">Henry Molaison</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levy-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> after what was thought to be the full removal of both his hippocampi. More recent examination of his brain, post-mortem, shows that the hippocampus was more intact than first thought, throwing theories drawn from the initial data into question. The hippocampus may be involved in changing neural connections for a period of three months or more after the initial learning.
</p><p>Research has suggested that long-term memory storage in humans may be maintained by <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">DNA methylation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller2007_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2007-35">[35]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">'prion' gene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further research investigated the molecular basis for <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memory</a>.  By 2015 it had become clear that long-term memory requires gene transcription activation and <a href="/wiki/De_novo_protein_synthesis_theory_of_memory_formation" title="De novo protein synthesis theory of memory formation">de novo protein synthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid25475090_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25475090-38">[38]</a></sup>  Long-term memory formation depends on both the activation of memory promoting genes and the inhibition of memory suppressor genes, and <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">DNA methylation</a>/<a href="/wiki/DNA_demethylation" title="DNA demethylation">DNA demethylation</a> was found to be a major mechanism for achieving this dual regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27634149_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27634149-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rats with a new, strong long-term memory due to <a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">contextual fear conditioning</a> have reduced expression of about 1,000 genes and increased expression of about 500 genes in the hippocampus 24 hours after training, thus exhibiting modified expression of 9.17% of the rat hippocampal genome.  Reduced gene expressions were associated with methylations of those genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28620075_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28620075-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Considerable further research into long-term memory has illuminated the molecular mechanisms by which methylations are established or removed, as reviewed in 2022.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-41">[41]</a></sup>  These mechanisms include, for instance, signal-responsive <a href="/wiki/TOP2B" title="TOP2B">TOP2B</a>-induced double-strand breaks in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immediate_early_genes" title="Immediate early genes">immediate early genes</a>.  Also the <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNAs</a> of many genes that had been subjected to methylation-controlled increases or decreases  are transported by neural granules (<a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNP" title="Messenger RNP">messenger RNP</a>) to the <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_spine" title="Dendritic spine">dendritic spines</a>. At these locations the messenger RNAs can be <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translated</a> into the proteins that control signaling at <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuronal</a> <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-store_model">Multi-store model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Multi-store model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Memory_consolidation" title="Memory consolidation">Memory consolidation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multistore_model.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="98" data-file-width="327" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Multistore_model.png/300px-Multistore_model.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Multistore_model.png 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multistore_model.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multi-store model</div></div></div>
<p>The multi-store model (also known as <a href="/wiki/Atkinson%E2%80%93Shiffrin_memory_model" title="Atkinson–Shiffrin memory model">Atkinson–Shiffrin memory model</a>) was first described in 1968 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Atkinson" title="Richard C. Atkinson">Atkinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Shiffrin" title="Richard Shiffrin">Shiffrin</a>.
</p><p>The multi-store model has been criticised for being too simplistic. For instance, long-term memory is believed to be actually made up of multiple subcomponents, such as episodic and <a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">procedural memory</a>. It also proposes that rehearsal is the only mechanism by which information eventually reaches long-term storage, but evidence shows us capable of remembering things without rehearsal.
</p><p>The model also shows all the memory stores as being a single unit whereas research into this shows differently. For example, short-term memory can be broken up into different units such as visual information and acoustic information. In a study by Zlonoga and Gerber (1986), patient 'KF' demonstrated certain deviations from the Atkinson–Shiffrin model. Patient KF was <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">brain damaged</a>, displaying difficulties regarding short-term memory. Recognition of sounds such as spoken numbers, letters, words and easily identifiable noises (such as doorbells and cats meowing) were all impacted. Visual short-term memory was unaffected, suggesting a dichotomy between visual and audial memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_memory">Working memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Working memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">Working memory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Working_memory_model.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="269" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Working_memory_model.svg/300px-Working_memory_model.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Working_memory_model.svg/450px-Working_memory_model.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Working_memory_model.svg/600px-Working_memory_model.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Working_memory_model.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The working memory model</div></div></div>
<p>In 1974 Baddeley and Hitch proposed a "working memory model" that replaced the general concept of short-term memory with an active maintenance of information in the short-term storage. In this model, working memory consists of three basic stores: the central executive, the phonological loop and the visuo-spatial sketchpad. In 2000 this model was expanded with the multimodal episodic buffer (<a href="/wiki/Baddeley%27s_model_of_working_memory" title="Baddeley's model of working memory">Baddeley's model of working memory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_2000_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_2000-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central executive essentially acts as an attention sensory store. It channels information to the three component processes: the phonological loop, the visuo-spatial sketchpad, and the episodic buffer.
</p><p>The phonological loop stores auditory information by silently rehearsing sounds or words in a continuous loop: the articulatory process (for example the repetition of a telephone number over and over again). A short list of data is easier to remember. The phonological loop is occasionally disrupted. <a href="/wiki/Irrelevant_speech_effect" title="Irrelevant speech effect">Irrelevant speech</a> or background noise can impede the phonological loop. <a href="/wiki/Articulatory_suppression" title="Articulatory suppression">Articulatory suppression</a> can also confuse encoding and words that sound similar can be switched or misremembered through the phonological similarity effect. the phonological loop also has a limit to how much it can hold at once which means that it is easier to remember a lot of short words rather than a lot of long words, according to the word length effect.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visuospatial_sketchpad" title="Visuospatial sketchpad">visuospatial sketchpad</a> stores visual and spatial information. It is engaged when performing spatial tasks (such as judging distances) or visual ones (such as counting the windows on a house or imagining images). Those with <a href="/wiki/Aphantasia" title="Aphantasia">Aphantasia</a> will not be able to engage the visuospatial sketchpad.
</p><p>The episodic buffer is dedicated to linking information across domains to form integrated units of visual, spatial, and verbal information and chronological ordering (e.g., the memory of a story or a movie scene). The episodic buffer is also assumed to have links to long-term memory and semantical meaning.
</p><p>The working memory model explains many practical observations, such as why it is easier to do two different tasks (one verbal and one visual) than two similar tasks (e.g., two visual), and the aforementioned word-length effect.  Working memory is also the premise for what allows us to do everyday activities involving thought. It is the section of memory where we carry out thought processes and use them to learn and reason about topics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_2000_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_2000-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Researchers distinguish between <b>recognition</b> and <b>recall</b> memory.  Recognition memory tasks require individuals to indicate whether they have encountered a stimulus (such as a picture or a word) before.  Recall memory tasks require participants to retrieve previously learned information.  For example, individuals might be asked  to produce a series of actions they have seen before or to say a list of words they have heard before.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_information_type">By information type</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: By information type">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Topographical memory</b> involves the ability to orient oneself in space, to recognize and follow an itinerary, or to recognize familiar places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Getting lost when traveling alone is an example of the failure of topographic memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aguirre_1999_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aguirre_1999-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Flashbulb memories</b> are clear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Episodic_memories" title="Episodic memories">episodic memories</a> of unique and highly emotional events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> People remembering where they were or what they were doing when they first heard the news of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Kennedy" title="President Kennedy">President Kennedy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">assassination</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neisser_1982_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neisser_1982-47">[47]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Sydney_hostage_crisis" title="2014 Sydney hostage crisis">Sydney Siege</a> or of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9/11" title="9/11">9/11</a> are examples of flashbulb memories.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Robert_Anderson_(psychologist)" title="John Robert Anderson (psychologist)">Anderson</a> (1976)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_1976_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_1976-48">[48]</a></sup> divides long-term memory into <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declarative_memory" title="Declarative memory">declarative (explicit)</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">procedural (implicit)</a></i> memories.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Declarative">Declarative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Declarative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declarative_memory" title="Declarative memory">Declarative memory</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Declarative_memory" title="Declarative memory">Declarative memory</a> requires <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conscious" title="Conscious">conscious</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recollection" title="Recollection">recall</a>, in that some conscious process must call back the information. It is sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">explicit memory</a></i>, since it consists of information that is explicitly stored and retrieved. Declarative memory can be further sub-divided into <a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">semantic memory</a>, concerning principles and facts taken independent of context; and <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a>, concerning information specific to a particular context, such as a time and place. Semantic memory allows the encoding of abstract <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> about the world, such as "Paris is the capital of France". Episodic memory, on the other hand, is used for more personal memories, such as the sensations, emotions, and personal associations of a particular place or time. Episodic memories often reflect the "firsts" in life such as a first kiss, first day of school or first time winning a championship. These are key events in one's life that can be remembered clearly.
</p><p>Research suggests that declarative memory is supported by several functions of the medial temporal lobe system which includes the hippocampus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Autobiographical_memory" title="Autobiographical memory">Autobiographical memory</a> – memory for particular events within one's own life – is generally viewed as either equivalent to, or a subset of, episodic memory. <a href="/wiki/Visual_memory" title="Visual memory">Visual memory</a> is part of memory preserving some characteristics of our senses pertaining to visual experience. One is able to place in memory information that resembles objects, places, animals or people in sort of a <a href="/wiki/Mental_image" title="Mental image">mental image</a>. Visual memory can result in <a href="/wiki/Priming_(psychology)" title="Priming (psychology)">priming</a> and it is assumed some kind of perceptual representational system underlies this phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Procedural">Procedural</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Procedural">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">procedural memory</a> (or <i><a href="/wiki/Implicit_memory" title="Implicit memory">implicit memory</a></i>) is not based on the conscious recall of information, but on <a href="/wiki/Implicit_learning" title="Implicit learning">implicit learning</a>. It can best be summarized as remembering how to do something. Procedural memory is primarily used in learning <a href="/wiki/Motor_skill" title="Motor skill">motor skills</a> and can be considered a subset of implicit memory. It is revealed when one does better in a given task due only to repetition – no new explicit memories have been formed, but one is <a href="/wiki/Unconscious_mind" title="Unconscious mind">unconsciously</a> accessing aspects of those previous experiences. Procedural memory involved in <a href="/wiki/Motor_learning" title="Motor learning">motor learning</a> depends on the <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basal_ganglia" title="Basal ganglia">basal ganglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A characteristic of procedural memory is that the things remembered are automatically translated into actions, and thus sometimes difficult to describe. Some examples of procedural memory include the ability to ride a bike or tie shoelaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schacter_2011_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schacter_2011-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_temporal_direction">By temporal direction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: By temporal direction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another major way to distinguish different memory functions is whether the content to be remembered is in the past, <a href="/wiki/Retrospective_memory" title="Retrospective memory">retrospective memory</a>, or in the future, <a href="/wiki/Prospective_memory" title="Prospective memory">prospective memory</a>. John Meacham introduced this distinction in a paper presented at the 1975 <a href="/wiki/American_Psychological_Association" title="American Psychological Association">American Psychological Association</a> annual meeting and subsequently included by <a href="/wiki/Ulric_Neisser" title="Ulric Neisser">Ulric Neisser</a> in his 1982 edited volume, <i>Memory Observed: Remembering in Natural Contexts</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Thus, retrospective memory as a category includes semantic, episodic  and autobiographical memory. In contrast, prospective memory is memory for future intentions, or <i>remembering to remember</i> (Winograd, 1988). Prospective memory can be further broken down into event- and time-based prospective remembering. Time-based prospective memories are triggered by a time-cue, such as going to the doctor (action) at 4pm (cue). Event-based prospective memories are intentions triggered by cues, such as remembering to post a letter (action) after seeing a mailbox (cue). Cues do not need to be related to the action (as the mailbox/letter example), and lists, sticky-notes, knotted handkerchiefs, or string around the finger all exemplify cues that people use as strategies to enhance prospective memory.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Study_techniques">Study techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Study techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="To_assess_infants">To assess infants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: To assess infants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Infants do not have the language ability to report on their memories and so verbal reports cannot be used to assess very young children's memory.  Throughout the years, however, researchers have adapted and developed a number of measures for assessing both infants' recognition memory and their recall memory.  <a href="/wiki/Habituation" title="Habituation">Habituation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">operant conditioning</a> techniques have been used to assess infants' recognition memory and the deferred and elicited imitation techniques have been used to assess infants' recall memory.
</p><p>Techniques used to assess infants' recognition memory include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Visual paired comparison procedure (relies on habituation)</b>: infants are first presented with pairs of visual stimuli, such as two black-and-white photos of human faces, for a fixed amount of time; then, after being familiarized with the two photos, they are presented with the "familiar" photo and a new photo.  The time spent looking at each photo is recorded.  Looking longer at the new photo indicates that they remember the "familiar" one.  Studies using this procedure have found that 5- to 6-month-olds can retain information for as long as fourteen days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fagan_1974_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fagan_1974-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Operant conditioning technique</b>: infants are placed in a crib and a ribbon that is connected to a mobile overhead is tied to one of their feet.  Infants notice that when they kick their foot the mobile moves – the rate of kicking increases dramatically within minutes.  Studies using this technique have revealed that infants' memory substantially improves over the first 18-months.  Whereas 2- to 3-month-olds can retain an operant response (such as activating the mobile by kicking their foot) for a week, 6-month-olds can retain it for two weeks, and 18-month-olds can retain a similar operant response for as long as 13 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rovee-Collier_1999_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rovee-Collier_1999-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rovee-Collier_1993_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rovee-Collier_1993-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartshorn_1998_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartshorn_1998-57">[57]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Techniques used to assess infants' recall memory include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Deferred imitation technique</b>: an experimenter shows infants a unique sequence of actions (such as using a stick to push a button on a box) and then, after a delay, asks the infants to imitate the actions.  Studies using deferred imitation have shown that 14-month-olds' memories for the sequence of actions can last for as long as four months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meltzoff_1995_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meltzoff_1995-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Elicited imitation technique</b>: is very similar to the deferred imitation technique; the difference is that infants are allowed to imitate the actions before the delay.  Studies using the elicited imitation technique have shown that 20-month-olds can recall the action sequences twelve months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer_2002_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer_2002-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer_2007_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer_2007-60">[60]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="To_assess_children_and_older_adults">To assess children and older adults</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: To assess children and older adults">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Researchers use a variety of tasks to assess older children and adults' memory. Some examples are:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Paired associate learning</b> – when one learns to associate one specific word with another. For example, when given a word such as  "safe" one must learn to say another specific word, such as "green". This is stimulus and response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_1_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_1-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kesner_2013_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kesner_2013-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Free recall</b> – during this task a subject would be asked to study a list of words and then later they will be asked to recall or write down as many words that they can remember, similar to free response questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_2_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_2-63">[63]</a></sup> Earlier items are affected by retroactive interference (RI), which means the longer the list, the greater the interference, and the less likelihood that they are recalled. On the other hand, items that have been presented lastly suffer little RI, but suffer a great deal from proactive interference (PI), which means the longer the delay in recall, the more likely that the items will be lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baddeley_1976_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baddeley_1976-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Cued recall</b> – one is given a significant hints to help retrieve information that has been previously encoded into the person's memory; typically this can involve a word relating to the information being asked to remember.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This is similar to fill in the blank assessments used in classrooms.</li>
<li><b>Recognition</b> – subjects are asked to remember a list of words or pictures, after which point they are asked to identify the previously presented words or pictures from among a list of alternatives that were not presented in the original list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_3_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica_3-66">[66]</a></sup> This is similar to multiple choice assessments.</li>
<li><b>Detection paradigm</b> – individuals are shown a number of objects and color samples during a certain period of time. They are then tested on their visual ability to remember as much as they can by looking at testers and pointing out whether the testers are similar to the sample, or if any change is present.</li>
<li><b>Savings method</b> – compares the speed of originally learning to the speed of relearning it. The amount of time saved measures memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Implicit-memory tasks</b> – information is drawn from memory without conscious realization.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Failures">Failures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Failures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Eyewitness_memory" title="Eyewitness memory">Eyewitness memory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2838" data-file-width="1892" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg/220px-Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg/330px-Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg/440px-Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Garten_des_Vergessens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The garden of oblivion, illustration by <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Moses_Lilien" title="Ephraim Moses Lilien">Ephraim Moses Lilien</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Transience</b> – memories degrade with the passing of time. This occurs in the storage stage of memory, after the information has been stored and before it is retrieved. This can happen in sensory, short-term, and long-term storage. It follows a general pattern where the information is rapidly forgotten during the first couple of days or years, followed by small losses in later days or years.</li>
<li><b>Absent-mindedness</b> – Memory failure due to the lack of <a href="/wiki/Attention" title="Attention">attention</a>. Attention plays a key role in storing information into long-term memory; without proper attention, the information might not be stored, making it impossible to be retrieved later.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brain areas involved in the <a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy_of_memory" title="Neuroanatomy of memory">neuroanatomy of memory</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a>, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammillary_bodies" title="Mammillary bodies">mammillary bodies</a> are thought to be involved in specific types of memory. For example, the hippocampus is believed to be involved in spatial learning and <a href="/wiki/Declarative_learning" title="Declarative learning">declarative learning</a>, while the amygdala is thought to be involved in <a href="/wiki/Emotion_and_memory" title="Emotion and memory">emotional memory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Damage to certain areas in patients and animal models and subsequent memory deficits is a primary source of information. However, rather than implicating a specific area, it could be that damage to adjacent areas, or to a pathway <i>traveling</i> through the area is actually responsible for the observed deficit. Further, it is not sufficient to describe memory, and its counterpart, <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a>, as solely dependent on specific brain regions. Learning and memory are usually attributed to changes in neuronal <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>, thought to be mediated by <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long-term_depression" title="Long-term depression">long-term depression</a>.
</p><p>In general, the more emotionally charged an event or experience is, the better it is remembered; this phenomenon is known as the <b>memory enhancement effect</b>.  Patients with amygdala damage, however, do not show a memory enhancement effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Donald_O._Hebb" title="Donald O. Hebb">Hebb</a> distinguished between short-term and long-term memory. He postulated that any memory that stayed in short-term storage for a long enough time would be consolidated into a long-term memory. Later research showed this to be false. Research has shown that direct injections of <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a> help the storage of recent experiences. This is also true for stimulation of the amygdala. This proves that excitement enhances memory by the stimulation of hormones that affect the amygdala. Excessive or prolonged stress (with prolonged cortisol) may hurt memory storage. Patients with amygdalar damage are no more likely to remember emotionally charged words than nonemotionally charged ones. The hippocampus is important for explicit memory. The hippocampus is also important for memory consolidation. The hippocampus receives input from different parts of the cortex and sends its output out to different parts of the brain also. The input comes from secondary and tertiary sensory areas that have processed the information a lot already. Hippocampal damage may also cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_loss" title="Memory loss">memory loss</a> and problems with memory storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> This memory loss includes <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_amnesia" title="Retrograde amnesia">retrograde amnesia</a> which is the loss of memory for events that occurred shortly before the time of brain damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_neuroscience">Cognitive neuroscience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cognitive neuroscience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cognitive neuroscientists consider memory as the retention, reactivation, and reconstruction of the experience-independent internal representation. The term of internal <a href="/wiki/Mental_representation" title="Mental representation">representation</a> implies that such a definition of memory contains two components: the expression of memory at the behavioral or conscious level, and the underpinning physical neural changes (Dudai 2007). The latter component is also called <a href="/wiki/Engram_(neuropsychology)" title="Engram (neuropsychology)">engram</a> or memory traces (Semon 1904). Some neuroscientists and psychologists mistakenly equate the concept of engram and memory, broadly conceiving all persisting after-effects of experiences as memory; others argue against this notion that memory does not exist until it is revealed in behavior or thought (Moscovitch 2007).
</p><p>One question that is crucial in cognitive neuroscience is how information and mental experiences are coded and represented in the brain. Scientists have gained much knowledge about the neuronal codes from the studies of plasticity, but most of such research has been focused on simple learning in simple neuronal circuits; it is considerably less clear about the neuronal changes involved in more complex examples of memory, particularly declarative memory that requires the storage of facts and events (Byrne 2007). <a href="/wiki/Convergence-divergence_zone" title="Convergence-divergence zone">Convergence-divergence zones</a> might be the neural networks where memories are stored and retrieved. Considering that there are several kinds of memory, depending on types of represented knowledge, underlying mechanisms, processes functions and modes of acquisition, it is likely that different brain areas support different memory systems and that they are in mutual relationships in neuronal networks: "components of memory representation are distributed widely across different parts of the brain as mediated by multiple neocortical circuits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crossingdialogues.com_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-crossingdialogues.com-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Encoding_(memory)" title="Encoding (memory)">Encoding</a>. Encoding of <a href="/wiki/Working_memory" title="Working memory">working memory</a> involves the spiking of individual neurons induced by sensory input, which persists even after the sensory input disappears (Jensen and Lisman 2005; Fransen et al. 2002). Encoding of <a href="/wiki/Episodic_memory" title="Episodic memory">episodic memory</a> involves persistent changes in molecular structures that alter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synaptic_transmission" title="Synaptic transmission">synaptic transmission</a> between neurons. Examples of such structural changes include <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a> (LTP) or <a href="/wiki/Spike-timing-dependent_plasticity" title="Spike-timing-dependent plasticity">spike-timing-dependent plasticity</a> (STDP).  The persistent spiking in working memory can enhance the synaptic and cellular changes in the encoding of episodic memory (Jensen and Lisman 2005).</li>
<li>Working memory. Recent functional imaging studies detected working memory signals in both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medial_temporal_lobe" title="Medial temporal lobe">medial temporal lobe</a> (MTL), a brain area strongly associated with <a href="/wiki/Long-term_memory" title="Long-term memory">long-term memory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a> (Ranganath et al. 2005), suggesting a strong relationship between working memory and long-term memory. However, the substantially more working memory signals seen in the prefrontal lobe suggest that this area plays a more important role in working memory than MTL (Suzuki 2007).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Memory_consolidation" title="Memory consolidation">Consolidation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconsolidation" title="Reconsolidation">reconsolidation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Short-term_memory" title="Short-term memory">Short-term memory</a> (STM) is temporary and subject to disruption, while long-term memory (LTM), once consolidated, is persistent and stable. Consolidation of STM into LTM at the molecular level presumably involves two processes: synaptic consolidation and system consolidation. The former involves a protein synthesis process in the medial temporal lobe (MTL), whereas the latter transforms the MTL-dependent memory into an MTL-independent memory over months to years (Ledoux 2007). In recent years, such traditional consolidation dogma has been re-evaluated as a result of the studies on reconsolidation. These studies showed that prevention after <a href="/wiki/Recall_(memory)" title="Recall (memory)">retrieval</a> affects subsequent retrieval of the memory (Sara 2000). New studies have shown that post-retrieval treatment with protein synthesis inhibitors and many other compounds can lead to an amnestic state (Nadel et al. 2000b; Alberini 2005; Dudai 2006).  These findings on reconsolidation fit with the behavioral evidence that retrieved memory is not a carbon copy of the initial experiences, and memories are updated during retrieval.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">Long-term potentiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Kandel" title="Eric Kandel">Eric Kandel</a></div>
<p>Study of the genetics of human memory is in its infancy though many genes have been investigated for their association to memory in humans and non-human animals. A notable initial success was the association of <a href="/wiki/Apolipoprotein_E" title="Apolipoprotein E">APOE</a> with memory dysfunction in <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>. The search for genes associated with normally varying memory continues. One of the first candidates for normal variation in memory is the protein <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KIBRA" title="KIBRA">KIBRA</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (September 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></i> which appears to be associated with the rate at which material is forgotten over a delay period. There has been some evidence that memories are stored in the nucleus of neurons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (September 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_underpinnings">Genetic underpinnings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Genetic underpinnings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several genes, proteins and enzymes have been extensively researched for their association with memory. Long-term memory, unlike short-term memory, is dependent upon the synthesis of new proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> This occurs within the cellular body, and concerns the particular transmitters, receptors, and new synapse pathways that reinforce the communicative strength between neurons. The production of new proteins devoted to synapse reinforcement is triggered after the release of certain signaling substances (such as calcium within hippocampal neurons) in the cell. In the case of hippocampal cells, this release is dependent upon the expulsion of magnesium (a binding molecule) that is expelled after significant and repetitive synaptic signaling. The temporary expulsion of magnesium frees NMDA receptors to release calcium in the cell, a signal that leads to gene transcription and the construction of reinforcing proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> For more information, see <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">long-term potentiation</a> (LTP).
</p><p>One of the newly synthesized proteins in LTP is also critical for maintaining long-term memory. This protein is an autonomously active form of the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_C" title="Protein kinase C">protein kinase C</a> (PKC), known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PKM%CE%B6" title="PKMζ">PKMζ</a>. PKMζ maintains the activity-dependent enhancement of synaptic strength and inhibiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PKM%CE%B6" title="PKMζ">PKMζ</a> erases established long-term memories, without affecting short-term memory or, once the inhibitor is eliminated, the ability to encode and store new long-term memories is restored. Also, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BDNF" title="BDNF">BDNF</a> is important for the persistence of long-term memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The long-term stabilization of synaptic changes is also determined by a parallel increase of pre- and postsynaptic structures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bouton_(synapse)" title="Bouton (synapse)">axonal bouton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dendritic_spine" title="Dendritic spine">dendritic spine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postsynaptic_density" title="Postsynaptic density">postsynaptic density</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stabilization_plasticity_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-stabilization_plasticity-78">[78]</a></sup>
On the molecular level, an increase of the postsynaptic scaffolding proteins <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSD-95" title="PSD-95">PSD-95</a> and <a href="/wiki/HOMER1" title="HOMER1">HOMER1c</a> has been shown to correlate with the stabilization of synaptic enlargement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stabilization_plasticity_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-stabilization_plasticity-78">[78]</a></sup> The cAMP response element-binding protein (<a href="/wiki/CREB" title="CREB">CREB</a>) is a <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a> which is believed to be important in consolidating short-term to long-term memories, and which is believed to be downregulated in <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_methylation_and_demethylation"><a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">DNA methylation</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA_demethylation" title="DNA demethylation">demethylation</a></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: DNA methylation and demethylation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rats exposed to an intense <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> event may retain a life-long memory of the event, even after a single training session.  The long-term memory of such an event appears to be initially stored in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a>, but this storage is transient.  Much of the long-term storage of the memory seems to take place in the <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cingulate_cortex" title="Anterior cingulate cortex">anterior cingulate cortex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>  When such an exposure was experimentally applied, more than 5,000 differently methylated DNA regions appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">hippocampus</a> <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuronal</a> <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of the rats at one and at 24 hours after training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>  These alterations in methylation pattern occurred at many <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> that were <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">down-regulated</a>, often due to the formation of new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-methylcytosine" title="5-methylcytosine">5-methylcytosine</a> sites in CpG rich regions of the genome.  Furthermore, many other genes were <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">upregulated</a>, likely often due to hypomethylation.  Hypomethylation often results from the removal of methyl groups from previously existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-methylcytosine" title="5-methylcytosine">5-methylcytosines</a> in DNA.  Demethylation is carried out by several proteins acting in concert, including the <a href="/wiki/TET_enzymes" title="TET enzymes">TET enzymes</a> as well as enzymes of the DNA <a href="/wiki/Base_excision_repair" title="Base excision repair">base excision repair</a> pathway (see <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics_in_learning_and_memory" title="Epigenetics in learning and memory">Epigenetics in learning and memory</a>).  The pattern of induced and repressed genes in brain neurons subsequent to an intense learning event likely provides the molecular basis for a long-term memory of the event.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epigenetics">Epigenetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Epigenetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics_in_learning_and_memory" title="Epigenetics in learning and memory">Epigenetics in learning and memory</a></div>
<p>Studies of the molecular basis for memory formation indicate that <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> mechanisms operating in <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> play a central role in determining this capability.  Key epigenetic mechanisms involved in memory include the <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">methylation</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA_demethylation" title="DNA demethylation">demethylation</a> of neuronal DNA, as well as modifications of <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histone</a> proteins including <a href="/wiki/Histone_methylation" title="Histone methylation">methylations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histone_acetylation_and_deacetylation" title="Histone acetylation and deacetylation">acetylations and deacetylations</a>.
</p><p>Stimulation of brain activity in memory formation is often accompanied by the generation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_damage" title="DNA damage">damage in neuronal DNA</a> that is followed by repair associated with persistent epigenetic alterations. In particular the DNA repair processes of <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Base_excision_repair" title="Base excision repair">base excision repair</a> are employed in memory formation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="removal of citation to content from predatory publisher (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_DNA_Topoisomerase_II_Beta_in_learning_and_memory">Role of DNA Topoisomerase II Beta in learning and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Role of DNA Topoisomerase II Beta in learning and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During a new <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> experience, a set of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> is rapidly expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>. This induced <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> is considered to be essential for processing the information being learned. Such genes are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Immediate_early_gene" title="Immediate early gene">immediate early genes</a> (IEGs). <a href="/wiki/TOP2B" title="TOP2B">DNA Topoisomerase II Beta (TOP2B)</a> activity is essential for the expression of IEGs in a type of learning experience in mice termed associative fear memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2019_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2019-82">[82]</a></sup> Such a learning experience appears to rapidly trigger TOP2B to induce double-strand breaks in the <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoter DNA</a> of IEG genes that function in <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neuroplasticity</a>. <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">Repair</a> of these induced breaks is associated with <a href="/wiki/DNA_demethylation" title="DNA demethylation">DNA demethylation</a> of IEG gene promoters allowing immediate expression of these IEG genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2019_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Li2019-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_(no_caption).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="910" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg/330px-Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg/495px-Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg/660px-Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_%28no_caption%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regulatory_sequence_in_a_promoter_at_a_transcription_start_site_with_a_paused_RNA_polymerase_(no_caption).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Regulatory sequence in a promoter at a transcription start site with a paused RNA polymerase and a TOP2B-induced double-strand break</div></div></div>
<p>The double-strand breaks that are induced during a learning experience are not immediately repaired.  About 600 regulatory sequences in <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoters</a> and about 800 regulatory sequences in <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_(genetics)" title="Enhancer (genetics)">enhancers</a> appear to depend on double strand breaks initiated by <a href="/wiki/TOP2B" title="TOP2B">topoisomerase 2-beta (TOP2B)</a> for activation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31110352_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31110352-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid32029477_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid32029477-84">[84]</a></sup>  The induction of particular double-strand breaks are specific with respect to their inducing signal.  When neurons are activated <i>in vitro</i>, just 22 of TOP2B-induced double-strand breaks occur in their genomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madabhushi_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madabhushi-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such TOP2B-induced double-strand breaks are accompanied by at least four enzymes of the <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining (NHEJ) DNA repair pathway</a> (DNA-PKcs, KU70, KU80, and DNA LIGASE IV) (see Figure). These enzymes repair the double-strand breaks within about 15 minutes to two hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madabhushi_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Madabhushi-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ju_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ju-86">[86]</a></sup>  The double-strand breaks in the promoter are thus associated with TOP2B and at least these four repair enzymes.  These proteins are present simultaneously on a single promoter <a href="/wiki/Nucleosome" title="Nucleosome">nucleosome</a> (there are about 147 nucleotides in the DNA sequence wrapped around a single nucleosome) located near the transcription start site of their target gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ju_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ju-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="889" data-file-width="1284" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg/330px-Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg/495px-Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg/660px-Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brain_regions_in_memory_formation_updated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brain regions involved in memory formation including medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC)</div></div></div>
<p>The double-strand break introduced by TOP2B apparently frees the part of the <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoter</a> at an RNA polymerase-bound <a href="/wiki/Transcription_preinitiation_complex" title="Transcription preinitiation complex">transcription start site</a> to physically move to its associated <a href="/wiki/Enhancer_(genetics)" title="Enhancer (genetics)">enhancer</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_sequence" title="Regulatory sequence">regulatory sequence</a>).  This allows the enhancer, with its bound <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mediator_(coactivator)" title="Mediator (coactivator)">mediator proteins</a>, to directly interact with the RNA polymerase paused at the <a href="/wiki/Transcription_preinitiation_complex" title="Transcription preinitiation complex">transcription start site</a> to start <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcription</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madabhushi_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Madabhushi-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen2015_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen2015-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fear_conditioning" title="Fear conditioning">Contextual fear conditioning</a> in the mouse causes the mouse to have a long-term memory and fear of the location in which it occurred.  Contextual fear conditioning causes hundreds of DSBs in mouse brain medial prefrontal cortex (mPFC) and hippocampus neurons (see Figure: Brain regions involved in memory formation).  These DSBs predominately activate genes involved in synaptic processes, that are important for learning and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_infancy">In infancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: In infancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the inability of adults to retrieve early memories, see <a href="/wiki/Childhood_amnesia" title="Childhood amnesia">Childhood amnesia</a>.</div>
