<p>Up until the mid-1980s it was assumed that infants could not encode, retain, and retrieve information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>  A growing body of research now indicates that infants as young as 6-months can recall information after a 24-hour delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170-90">[90]</a></sup>  Furthermore, research has revealed that as infants grow older they can store information for longer periods of time; 6-month-olds can recall information after a 24-hour period, 9-month-olds after up to five weeks, and 20-month-olds after as long as twelve months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>  In addition, studies have shown that with age, infants can store information faster.  Whereas 14-month-olds can recall a three-step sequence after being exposed to it once, 6-month-olds need approximately six exposures in order to be able to remember it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meltzoff_1995_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meltzoff_1995-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although 6-month-olds can recall information over the short-term, they have difficulty recalling the temporal order of information.  It is only by 9 months of age that infants can recall the actions of a two-step sequence in the correct temporal order – that is, recalling step 1 and then step 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer_2003_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer_2003-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carver_1999_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carver_1999-93">[93]</a></sup> In other words, when asked to imitate a two-step action sequence (such as putting a toy car in the base and pushing in the plunger to make the toy roll to the other end), 9-month-olds tend to imitate the actions of the sequence in the correct order (step 1 and then step 2).  Younger infants (6-month-olds) can only recall one step of a two-step sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barr_R.,_Dowden_A.,_&amp;_Hayne_H._1996_159–170-90">[90]</a></sup> Researchers have suggested that these age differences are probably due to the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Dentate_gyrus" title="Dentate gyrus">dentate gyrus</a> of the hippocampus and the frontal components of the neural network are not fully developed at the age of 6-months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer_2002_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer_2002-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carver_2001_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carver_2001-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, the term 'infantile amnesia' refers to the phenomenon of accelerated forgetting during infancy. Importantly, infantile amnesia is not unique to humans, and preclinical research (using rodent models) provides insight into the precise neurobiology of this phenomenon. A review of the literature from behavioral neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Jee_Hyun_Kim" title="Jee Hyun Kim">Dr Jee Hyun Kim</a> suggests that accelerated forgetting during early life is at least partly due to rapid growth of the brain during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aging">Aging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Aging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Memory_and_aging" title="Memory and aging">Memory and aging</a></div>
<p>One of the key concerns of older adults is the experience of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_loss" title="Memory loss">memory loss</a>, especially as it is one of the hallmark symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>. However, memory loss is qualitatively different in normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aging" title="Aging">aging</a> from the kind of memory loss associated with a diagnosis of Alzheimer's (Budson &amp; Price, 2005).  Research has revealed that individuals' performance on memory tasks that rely on frontal regions declines with age.  Older adults tend to exhibit deficits on tasks that involve knowing the temporal order in which they learned information;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> source memory tasks that require them to remember the specific circumstances or context in which they learned information;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and prospective memory tasks that involve remembering to perform an act at a future time.  Older adults can manage their problems with prospective memory by using appointment books, for example.
</p><p>Gene <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(biology)" title="Transcription (biology)">transcription</a> profiles were determined for the human <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">frontal cortex</a> of individuals from age 26 to 106 years.  Numerous genes were identified with reduced expression after age 40, and especially after age 70.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15190254_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15190254-99">[99]</a></sup>  Genes that play central roles in memory and <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> were among those showing the most significant reduction with age.  There was also a marked increase in <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a>, likely <a href="/wiki/DNA_oxidation" title="DNA oxidation">oxidative damage</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoters</a> of those genes with reduced expression.  It was suggested  that DNA damage may reduce the expression of selectively vulnerable genes involved in memory and learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15190254_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15190254-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorders">Disorders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Disorders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Memory_disorder" title="Memory disorder">Memory disorder</a></div>
<p>Much of the current knowledge of memory has come from studying <i>memory disorders</i>, particularly loss of memory, known as <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>. Amnesia can result from extensive damage to: (a) the regions of the medial temporal lobe, such as the hippocampus, dentate gyrus, subiculum, amygdala, the parahippocampal, entorhinal, and perirhinal cortices<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> or the (b) midline diencephalic region, specifically the dorsomedial nucleus of the thalamus and the mammillary bodies of the hypothalamus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>  There are many sorts of amnesia, and by studying their different forms, it has become possible to observe apparent defects in individual sub-systems of the brain's memory systems, and thus hypothesize their function in the normally working brain. Other <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurological</a> disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (September 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> can also affect memory and cognition.
<a href="/wiki/Hyperthymesia" title="Hyperthymesia">Hyperthymesia</a>, or hyperthymesic syndrome, is a disorder that affects an individual's autobiographical memory, essentially meaning that they cannot forget small details that otherwise would not be stored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (September 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korsakoff%27s_syndrome" title="Korsakoff's syndrome">Korsakoff's syndrome</a>, also known as Korsakoff's psychosis, amnesic-confabulatory syndrome, is an organic brain disease that adversely affects memory by widespread loss or shrinkage of neurons within the prefrontal cortex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>While not a disorder, a common <i>temporary</i> failure of word retrieval from memory is the <a href="/wiki/Tip_of_the_tongue" title="Tip of the tongue">tip-of-the-tongue</a> <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomenon</a>. Those with <a href="/wiki/Anomic_aphasia" title="Anomic aphasia">Anomic aphasia</a> (also called Nominal aphasia or Anomia), however, do experience the tip-of-the-tongue phenomenon on an ongoing basis due to damage to the frontal and parietal <a href="/wiki/Lobes_of_the_brain" title="Lobes of the brain">lobes of the brain</a>.
</p><p>Memory dysfunction can also occur after viral infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-104">[104]</a></sup> Many patients recovering from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impact_of_COVID-19_on_neurological,_psychological_and_other_mental_health_outcomes" title="Impact of COVID-19 on neurological, psychological and other mental health outcomes">COVID-19 experience memory lapses</a>. Other viruses can also elicit memory dysfunction, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome_coronavirus" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus">SARS-CoV-1</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_East_respiratory_syndrome%E2%80%93related_coronavirus" title="Middle East respiratory syndrome–related coronavirus">MERS-CoV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaire_ebolavirus" title="Zaire ebolavirus">Ebola virus</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Orthomyxoviridae" title="Orthomyxoviridae">influenza virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influencing_factors">Influencing factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Influencing factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Interference can hamper memorization and retrieval. There is <a href="/wiki/Interference_theory" title="Interference theory">retroactive interference</a>, when learning new information makes it harder to recall old information<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and <b>proactive interference</b>, where prior learning disrupts recall of new information.  Although interference can lead to forgetting, it is important to keep in mind that there are situations when old information can facilitate learning of new information.  Knowing Latin, for instance, can help an individual learn a related language such as French – this phenomenon is known as positive transfer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perkins_Salomon_1992_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perkins_Salomon_1992-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Stress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_stress_on_memory" title="Effects of stress on memory">Effects of stress on memory</a></div>
<p>Stress has a significant effect on memory formation and learning.  In response to stressful situations, the brain releases hormones and neurotransmitters (ex. glucocorticoids and catecholamines) which affect memory encoding processes in the hippocampus.  Behavioural research on animals shows that chronic stress produces adrenal hormones which impact the hippocampal structure in the brains of rats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>  An experimental study by German cognitive psychologists L. Schwabe and O. Wolf demonstrates how learning under stress also decreases memory recall in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>  In this study, 48 healthy female and male university students participated in either a stress test or a control group.  Those randomly assigned to the stress test group had a hand immersed in ice cold water (the reputable SECPT or 'Socially Evaluated Cold Pressor Test') for up to three minutes, while being monitored and videotaped.   Both the stress and control groups were then presented with 32 words to memorize.  Twenty-four hours later, both groups were tested to see how many words they could remember (free recall) as well as how many they could recognize from a larger list of words (recognition performance). The results showed a clear impairment of memory performance in the stress test group, who recalled 30% fewer words than the control group.  The researchers suggest that stress experienced during learning distracts people by diverting their attention during the memory encoding process.
</p><p>However, memory performance can be enhanced when material is linked to the learning context, even when learning occurs under stress.  A separate study by cognitive psychologists Schwabe and Wolf shows that when retention testing is done in a context similar to or congruent with the original learning task (i.e., in the same room), memory impairment and the detrimental effects of stress on learning can be attenuated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Seventy-two healthy female and male university students, randomly assigned to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SECPT_stress_test" title="SECPT stress test">SECPT stress test</a> or to a control group, were asked to remember the locations of 15 pairs of picture cards – a computerized version of the card game "Concentration" or "Memory".  The room in which the experiment took place was infused with the scent of vanilla, as odour is a strong cue for memory.  Retention testing took place the following day, either in the same room with the vanilla scent again present, or in a different room without the fragrance.    The memory performance of subjects who experienced stress during the object-location task decreased significantly when they were tested in an unfamiliar room without the vanilla scent (an incongruent context);  however, the memory performance of stressed subjects showed no impairment when they were tested in the original room with the vanilla scent (a congruent context). All participants in the experiment, both stressed and unstressed, performed faster when the learning and retrieval contexts were similar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>This research on the effects of stress on memory may have practical implications for education, for <a href="/wiki/Eyewitness_testimony" title="Eyewitness testimony">eyewitness testimony</a> and for psychotherapy:  students may perform better when tested in their regular classroom rather than an exam room, eyewitnesses may recall details better at the scene of an event than in a courtroom, and persons with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress" title="Post-traumatic stress">post-traumatic stress</a> may improve when helped to situate their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memories_of_a_traumatic_event" title="Memories of a traumatic event">memories of a traumatic event</a> in an appropriate context.
</p><p>Stressful life experiences may be a cause of memory loss as a person ages. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" title="Glucocorticoids">Glucocorticoids</a> that are released during stress cause damage to neurons that are located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocampal" title="Hippocampal">hippocampal</a> region of the brain. Therefore, the more stressful situations that someone encounters, the more susceptible they are to memory loss later on. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CA1_neurons" title="CA1 neurons">CA1 neurons</a> found in the hippocampus are destroyed due to glucocorticoids decreasing the release of glucose and the reuptake of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a>. This high level of extracellular glutamate allows calcium to enter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMDA_receptors" title="NMDA receptors">NMDA receptors</a> which in return kills neurons. Stressful life experiences can also cause repression of memories where a person moves an unbearable memory to the unconscious mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> This directly relates to traumatic events in one's past such as kidnappings, being prisoners of war or sexual abuse as a child.
</p><p>The more long term the exposure to stress is, the more impact it may have. However, short term exposure to stress also causes impairment in memory by interfering with the function of the hippocampus. Research shows that subjects placed in a stressful situation for a short amount of time still have blood glucocorticoid levels that have increased drastically when measured after the exposure is completed. When subjects are asked to complete a learning task after short term exposure they often have difficulties. Prenatal stress also hinders the ability to learn and memorize by disrupting the development of the hippocampus and can lead to unestablished long term potentiation in the offspring of severely stressed parents. Although the stress is applied prenatally, the offspring show increased levels of glucocorticoids when they are subjected to stress later on in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> One explanation for why children from lower socioeconomic backgrounds tend to display poorer memory performance than their higher-income peers is the effects of stress accumulated over the course of the lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-113">[113]</a></sup> The effects of low income on the developing hippocampus is also thought be mediated by chronic stress responses which may explain why children from lower and higher-income backgrounds differ in terms of memory performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sleep">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_and_memory" title="Sleep and memory">Sleep and memory</a></div>
<p>Making memories occurs through a three-step process, which can be enhanced by <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>. The three steps are as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>Acquisition which is the process of storage and retrieval of new information in memory</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Memory_consolidation" title="Memory consolidation">Consolidation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Recall_(memory)" title="Recall (memory)">Recall</a></li></ol>
<p>Sleep affects memory consolidation. During sleep, the neural connections in the brain are strengthened. This enhances the brain's abilities to stabilize and retain memories. There have been several studies which show that sleep improves the retention of memory, as memories are enhanced through active consolidation. System consolidation takes place during slow-wave sleep (SWS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sleep_Deprivation_and_Memory_Loss_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sleep_Deprivation_and_Memory_Loss-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (September 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This process implicates that memories are reactivated during sleep, but that the process doesn't enhance every memory.  It also implicates that qualitative changes are made to the memories when they are transferred to long-term store during sleep. During sleep, the hippocampus replays the events of the day for the neocortex. The neocortex then reviews and processes memories, which moves them into long-term memory. When one does not get enough sleep it makes it more difficult to learn as these neural connections are not as strong, resulting in a lower retention rate of memories. Sleep deprivation makes it harder to focus, resulting in inefficient learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sleep_Deprivation_and_Memory_Loss_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sleep_Deprivation_and_Memory_Loss-114">[114]</a></sup> Furthermore, some studies have shown that sleep deprivation can lead to false memories as the memories are not properly transferred to long-term memory.
One of the primary functions of sleep is thought to be the improvement of the consolidation of information, as several studies have demonstrated that memory depends on getting sufficient sleep between training and test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellenbogen_2006_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellenbogen_2006-115">[115]</a></sup> Additionally, data obtained from neuroimaging studies have shown activation patterns in the sleeping brain that mirror those recorded during the learning of tasks from the previous day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellenbogen_2006_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellenbogen_2006-115">[115]</a></sup> suggesting that new memories may be solidified through such rehearsal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction_for_general_manipulation">Construction for general manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Construction for general manipulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although people often think that memory operates like recording equipment, this is not the case. The molecular mechanisms underlying the induction and maintenance of memory are very dynamic and comprise distinct phases covering a time window from seconds to even a lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In fact, research has revealed that our memories are constructed: "current hypotheses suggest that constructive processes allow individuals to simulate and imagine future episodes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> happenings, and scenarios. Since the future is not an exact repetition of the past, simulation of future episodes requires a complex system that can draw on the past in a manner that flexibly extracts and recombines elements of previous experiences – a constructive rather than a reproductive system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crossingdialogues.com_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-crossingdialogues.com-72">[72]</a></sup> People can construct their memories when they encode them and/or when they recall them.  To illustrate, consider a classic study conducted by Elizabeth Loftus and John Palmer (1974)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> in which people were instructed to watch a film of a traffic accident and then asked about what they saw.  The researchers found that the people who were asked, "How fast were the cars going when they <i>smashed</i> into each other?" gave higher estimates than those who were asked, "How fast were the cars going when they <i>hit</i> each other?"  Furthermore, when asked a week later whether they had seen broken glass in the film, those who had been asked the question with <i>smashed</i> were twice more likely to report that they had seen broken glass than those who had been asked the question with <i>hit</i> (there was no broken glass depicted in the film).  Thus, the wording of the questions distorted viewers' memories of the event.  Importantly, the wording of the question led people to construct different memories of the event – those who were asked the question with <i>smashed</i> recalled a more serious car accident than they had actually seen.  The findings of this experiment were replicated around the world, and researchers consistently demonstrated that when people were provided with misleading information they tended to misremember, a phenomenon known as the <a href="/wiki/Misinformation_effect" title="Misinformation effect">misinformation effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research has revealed that asking individuals to repeatedly imagine actions that they have never performed or events that they have never experienced could result in false memories.  For instance, Goff and Roediger<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> (1998) asked participants to imagine that they performed an act (e.g., break a toothpick) and then later asked them whether they had done such a thing.  Findings revealed that those participants who repeatedly imagined performing such an act were more likely to think that they had actually performed that act during the first session of the experiment.  Similarly, Garry and her colleagues (1996)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> asked college students to report how certain they were that they experienced a number of events as children (e.g., broke a window with their hand) and then two weeks later asked them to imagine four of those events.  The researchers found that one-fourth of the students asked to imagine the four events reported that they had actually experienced such events as children.  That is, when asked to imagine the events they were more confident that they experienced the events.
</p><p>Research reported in 2013 revealed that it is possible to artificially stimulate prior memories and artificially implant false memories in mice. Using <a href="/wiki/Optogenetics" title="Optogenetics">optogenetics</a>, a team of RIKEN-MIT scientists caused the mice to incorrectly associate a benign environment with a prior unpleasant experience from different surroundings. Some scientists believe that the study may have implications in studying false memory formation in humans, and in treating PTSD and <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20130725_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20130725-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (September 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Memory reconsolidation is when previously consolidated memories are recalled or retrieved from long-term memory to your active consciousness. During this process, memories can be further strengthened and added to but there is also risk of manipulation involved. We like to think of our memories as something stable and constant when they are stored in long-term memory but this isn't the case. There are a large number of studies that found that consolidation of memories is not a singular event but are put through the process again, known as reconsolidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-125">[125]</a></sup> This is when a memory is recalled or retrieved and placed back into your working memory. The memory is now open to manipulation from outside sources and the misinformation effect which could be due to misattributing the source of the inconsistent information, with or without an intact original memory trace (Lindsay and Johnson, 1989).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> One thing that can be sure is that memory is malleable.
</p><p>This new research into the concept of reconsolidation has opened the door to methods to help those with unpleasant memories or those that struggle with memories. An example of this is if you had a truly frightening experience and recall that memory in a less arousing environment, the memory will be weaken the next time it is retrieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-:03-125">[125]</a></sup>  "Some studies suggest that over-trained or strongly reinforced memories do not undergo reconsolidation if reactivated the first few days after training, but do become sensitive to reconsolidation interference with time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-:03-125">[125]</a></sup> This, however does not mean that all memory is susceptible to reconsolidation. There is evidence to suggest that memory that has undergone strong training and whether or not is it intentional is less likely to undergo reconsolidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> There was further testing done with rats and mazes that showed that reactivated memories were more susceptible to manipulation, in both good and bad ways, than newly formed memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> It is still not known whether or not these are new memories formed and it's an inability to retrieve the proper one for the situation or if it's a reconsolidated memory. Because the study of reconsolidation is still a newer concept, there is still debate on whether it should be considered scientifically sound.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Improving">Improving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Improving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Improving_memory" title="Improving memory">Improving memory</a></div>
<p>A UCLA research study published in the June 2008 issue of the <i>American Journal of Geriatric Psychiatry</i> found that people can improve <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive function</a> and brain efficiency through simple lifestyle changes such as incorporating memory exercises, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healthy_eating" title="Healthy eating">healthy eating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">stress reduction</a> into their daily lives. This study examined 17 subjects, (average age 53) with normal memory performance.
Eight subjects were asked to follow a "brain healthy" diet, relaxation, physical, and mental exercise (brain teasers and verbal memory training techniques). After 14 days, they showed greater word fluency (not memory) compared to their baseline performance. No long-term follow-up was conducted; it is therefore unclear if this intervention has lasting effects on memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small_2006_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Small_2006-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a loosely associated group of mnemonic principles and techniques that can be used to vastly improve memory known as the <a href="/wiki/Art_of_memory" title="Art of memory">art of memory</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Longevity_Center" title="International Longevity Center">International Longevity Center</a> released in 2001 a report<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> which includes in pages 14–16 recommendations for keeping the mind in good functionality until advanced age. Some of the recommendations are:
</p>
<ul><li>to stay intellectually active through learning</li>
<li>training or reading</li>
<li>to keep physically active so to promote blood circulation to the brain</li>
<li>to socialize</li>
<li>to reduce stress</li>
<li>to keep sleep time regular</li>
<li>to avoid depression or emotional instability</li>
<li>to observe good nutrition.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Memorization" title="Memorization">Memorization</a> is a method of learning that allows an individual to recall information verbatim. <a href="/wiki/Rote_learning" title="Rote learning">Rote learning</a> is the method most often used. Methods of memorizing things have been the subject of much discussion over the years with some writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Cosmos_Rossellius" title="Cosmos Rossellius">Cosmos Rossellius</a> using visual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphabets" title="Alphabets">alphabets</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spacing_effect" title="Spacing effect">spacing effect</a> shows that an individual is more likely to remember a list of items when rehearsal is spaced over an extended period of time. In contrast to this is <a href="/wiki/Cram_school" title="Cram school">cramming</a>: an intensive memorization in a short period of time. the spacing effect is exploited to improve memory in <a href="/wiki/Spaced_repetition" title="Spaced repetition">spaced repetition</a> flashcard training. Also relevant is the <a href="/wiki/Zeigarnik_effect" title="Zeigarnik effect">Zeigarnik effect</a> which states that people remember uncompleted or interrupted tasks better than completed ones. The so-called <a href="/wiki/Method_of_loci" title="Method of loci">Method of loci</a> uses spatial memory to memorize non-spatial information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_plants">In plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: In plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Plants lack a specialized organ devoted to memory retention, so plant memory has been a controversial topic in recent years. New advances in the field have identified the presence of <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitters</a> in plants, adding to the hypothesis that plants are capable of remembering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">Action potentials</a>, a physiological response characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, have been shown to have an influence on plants as well, including in wound responses and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-132">[132]</a></sup> In addition to these homologous features of memory systems in both plants and animals, plants have also been observed to encode, store and retrieve basic short-term memories.
</p><p>One of the most well-studied plants to show rudimentary memory is the <a href="/wiki/Venus_flytrap" title="Venus flytrap">Venus flytrap</a>. Native to the subtropical wetlands of the eastern United States, Venus Fly Traps have evolved the ability to obtain meat for sustenance, likely due to the lack of nitrogen in the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-133">[133]</a></sup> This is done by two trap-forming leaf tips that snap shut once triggered by a potential prey. On each lobe, three triggers hairs await stimulation. In order to maximize the benefit to cost ratio, the plant enables a rudimentary form of memory in which two trigger hairs must be stimulated within 30 seconds in order to result in trap closure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-133">[133]</a></sup> This system ensures that the trap only closes when potential prey is within grasp.
</p><p>The time lapse between trigger hair stimulations suggests that the plant can remember an initial stimulus long enough for a second stimulus to initiate trap closure. This memory isn't encoded in a brain, as plants lack this specialized organ. Rather, information is stored in the form of cytoplasmic calcium levels. The first trigger causes a subthreshold cytoplasmic calcium influx.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-133">[133]</a></sup> This initial trigger isn't enough to activate trap closure, so a subsequent stimulus allows for a secondary influx of calcium. The latter calcium rise superimposes on the initial one, creating an action potential that passes threshold, resulting in trap closure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-133">[133]</a></sup> Researchers, to prove that an electrical threshold must be met to stimulate trap closure, excited a single trigger hair with a constant mechanical stimulus using Ag/AgCl electrodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-134">[134]</a></sup> The trap closed after only a few seconds. This experiment gave evidence to demonstrate that the electrical threshold, not necessarily the number of trigger hair stimulations, was the contributing factor in Venus Fly Trap memory. It has been shown that trap closure can be blocked using uncouplers and inhibitors of <a href="/wiki/Voltage-gated_ion_channel" title="Voltage-gated ion channel">voltage-gated channels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-134">[134]</a></sup> After trap closure, these electrical signals stimulate glandular production of <a href="/wiki/Jasmonic_acid" title="Jasmonic acid">jasmonic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrolase" title="Hydrolase">hydrolases</a>, allowing for digestion of the prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>The field of plant neurobiology has gained a large amount of interest over the past decade, leading to an influx of research regarding plant memory. Although the Venus flytrap is one of the more highly studied, many other plants exhibit the capacity to remember, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Mimosa_pudica" title="Mimosa pudica">Mimosa pudica</a></i> through an experiment conducted by Monica Gagliano and colleagues in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gagliano2014_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gagliano2014-136">[136]</a></sup> To study the <i><a href="/wiki/Mimosa_pudica" title="Mimosa pudica">Mimosa pudica</a>,</i> Gagliano designed an apparatus with which potted mimosa plants could be repeatedly dropped the same distance and at the same speed. It was observed that the plants defensive response of curling up its leaves decreased over the 60 times the experiment was repeated per plant. To confirm that this was a mechanism of memory rather than exhaustion, some of the plants were shaken post experiment and displayed normal defensive responses of leaf curling. This experiment also demonstrated long-term memory in the plants, as it was repeated a month later and the plants were observed to remain unfazed by the dropping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gagliano2014_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gagliano2014-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Prenatal_memory" title="Prenatal memory">Prenatal memory</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Procedural_memory" title="Procedural memory">Procedural memory</a>, a type most frequently below conscious awareness that helps perform particular types of action</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sensory_memory" title="Sensory memory">Sensory memory</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Memory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">

<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFEck2014">Eck A (June 3, 2014). <a class="external text" href="https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/next/body/taking-notes-by-hand-could-improve-memory-wt/" rel="nofollow">"For More Effective Studying, Take Notes With Pen and Paper"</a>. <i>Nova Next</i>. PBS.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nova+Next&amp;rft.atitle=For+More+Effective+Studying%2C+Take+Notes+With+Pen+and+Paper&amp;rft.date=2014-06-03&amp;rft.aulast=Eck&amp;rft.aufirst=A&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.pbs.org%2Fwgbh%2Fnova%2Fnext%2Fbody%2Ftaking-notes-by-hand-could-improve-memory-wt%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMemory"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFFernyhough2013">Fernyhough C (2013). <i>Pieces of Light: How the New Science of Memory Illuminates Stories We Tell About Our Pasts</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-06-223789-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-06-223789-7"><bdi>978-0-06-223789-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Pieces+of+Light%3A+How+the+New+Science+of+Memory+Illuminates+Stories+We+Tell+About+Our+Pasts&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-06-223789-7&amp;rft.aulast=Fernyhough&amp;rft.aufirst=C&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMemory"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFFields2020">Fields RD (March 2020). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8284127" rel="nofollow">"The Brain Learns in Unexpected Ways: Neuroscientists have discovered a set of unfamiliar cellular mechanisms for making fresh memories"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>. <b>322</b> (3): 74–79. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8284127" rel="nofollow">8284127</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/34276078" rel="nofollow">34276078</a>. <q><a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">Myelin</a>, long considered inert insulation on <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a>, is now seen as making a contribution to learning by controlling the speed at which signals travel along neural wiring.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Scientific+American&amp;rft.atitle=The+Brain+Learns+in+Unexpected+Ways%3A+Neuroscientists+have+discovered+a+set+of+unfamiliar+cellular+mechanisms+for+making+fresh+memories&amp;rft.volume=322&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=74-79&amp;rft.date=2020-03&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC8284127%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F34276078&amp;rft.aulast=Fields&amp;rft.aufirst=RD&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC8284127&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMemory"></span></li>
